<div id="review_2344080887" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2344080887" />
    <a title="Colleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30274173-colleen"><img alt="Colleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589824510p2/30274173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2344080887?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Colleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Colleen" href="/user/show/30274173-colleen">Colleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30274173-colleen?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30274173-colleen?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30274173-colleen?shelf=books-for-my-map">books-for-my-map</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30274173-colleen?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30274173-colleen?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30274173-colleen?shelf=short-stories-novellas">short-stories-novellas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2344080887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6014394632299883080"><b>3 Stars</b><br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9057.Gaston_Leroux' title='Gaston Leroux' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gaston Leroux</a> is best known for writing <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/480204.The_Phantom_of_the_Opera' title='The Phantom of the Opera by Gaston Leroux' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Phantom of the Opera</a>. Apparently he also wrote detective fiction, and his character of Joseph Rouletabille is to France what <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3590.The_Adventures_of_Sherlock_Holmes' title='The Adventures of Sherlock Holmes by Arthur Conan Doyle' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sherlock Holmes</a> is to England.<br/><br/>I'll get the comparisons to Sherlock Holmes out of the way first. It is inevitable that the two are compared. Since <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/102868.A_Study_in_Scarlet' title='A Study in Scarlet by Arthur Conan Doyle' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Study in Scarlet</a> was first published in 1887 and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/188214.The_Mystery_of_the_Yellow_Room' title='The Mystery of the Yellow Room by Gaston Leroux' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Mystery of the Yellow Room</a> was not published until 1907, it is clear who came up with the idea first. I have no idea whethe</span>
  <span id="freeText6014394632299883080" style="display:none"><b>3 Stars</b><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9057.Gaston_Leroux" title="Gaston Leroux" rel="nofollow noopener">Gaston Leroux</a> is best known for writing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/480204.The_Phantom_of_the_Opera" title="The Phantom of the Opera by Gaston Leroux" rel="nofollow noopener">The Phantom of the Opera</a>. Apparently he also wrote detective fiction, and his character of Joseph Rouletabille is to France what <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3590.The_Adventures_of_Sherlock_Holmes" title="The Adventures of Sherlock Holmes by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">Sherlock Holmes</a> is to England.<br /><br />I'll get the comparisons to Sherlock Holmes out of the way first. It is inevitable that the two are compared. Since <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/102868.A_Study_in_Scarlet" title="A Study in Scarlet by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">A Study in Scarlet</a> was first published in 1887 and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/188214.The_Mystery_of_the_Yellow_Room" title="The Mystery of the Yellow Room by Gaston Leroux" rel="nofollow noopener">The Mystery of the Yellow Room</a> was not published until 1907, it is clear who came up with the idea first. I have no idea whether or not Leroux ever read Sherlock Holmes, but since I have I could not help thinking of the latter while reading <i>The Mystery of the Yellow Room</i>. Both series star a person who solves crimes through deductions and far outsmarts every else around. Both series are told through the narration of a sidekick who loves to sing their praise. There are similarities in the story setup and writing as well. But I definitely like Sherlock better than Joseph Rouletabille.<br /><br /><i>The Mystery of the Yellow Room</i> was Leroux's first book, so I was willing to give leeway for some things. But I was surprised that this is considered one of the greatest locked room mysteries ever written. It was decent but not astounding. The circumstances surrounding the events and the revelation of how the crime was committed all felt contrived. It definitely was not a mystery that readers could play along with. Meaning, revelations were based on things the reader was not shown, so it was impossible to deduct anything. And for a novella, it did drag on a bit.<br /><br />The novella is written in the old style of serial publication which made it fragmented sometimes. (To be fair, it was the popular style when this book was written.) But it does contain some dense, run-on paragraphs. The popular trend was also for chapter headings to have teasers (and sometimes spoilers). Much is revealed ahead of time as if guarding the readers' delicate sensibilities from too much shock. <br /><br />The main character is Joseph Rouletabille, a young reporter who is apparently smarter than everyone else and the only one capable of figuring out crimes. He started his dazzling journalistic career at age sixteen. Since this was the early 1900's, it was not surprising for a sixteen-year-old to be working. What was surprising was that a mere two years later (when this story takes place), Rouletabille already has an established career and has made himself indispensable not only to his newspaper but also to the police and justice system. And so this Mary Sue reporter (or Gary Stu if you prefer the male moniker) is granted <i>carte blanche</i> access to crime scenes because he is the only one who could possibly solve any mystery. He is accompanied by his lawyer sidekick (whose name I have already forgotten) who is so awed by his younger friend's astounding intellect that he feels the world must be told of his great deeds. The character development is certainly lacking. There are many references to Rouletabille's mysterious past, but all the reader gets is a pat on the head and <i>"maybe it will be revealed in the next book. Wink, wink."</i> And none of the other characters matter beyond their involvement in the mystery. Another complaint was that the only female character is a helpless damsel in distress with no personality.<br /><br />There is a heavy emphasis placed on the importance of truth over justice. Rouletabille blatantly states that he does not care about "justice;" he cares about the truth being known. He is a reporter after all. Since the author was also a journalist, that seemed to be a very deliberate point. Rouletabille wanted the perpetrator to be known but stated that it was not his problem whether or not that person was brought to justice. I have no idea what the laws in France in 1907 were, but you would think his lawyer friend would have made some attempt to ensure that due process was followed. Basically, this is the story of an overly-smart teenage reporter running amuck of the justice system and solving mysteries to satisfy his own curiosity.<br /><br />Another note: the edition I read had a translation footnote near the beginning of the story that contained a spoiler for the main plot. It was annoying to have something given away like that.<br /><br />I won't give away the big reveal. But Rouletabille does everything short of swing in on a chandelier to make it was dramatic as possible. Then the story ends with a blatant teaser for the next book. I will probably try out the next book in the series to see if it improves on the first one. But this only an average book to me.<br /><br /><br /><b>RATING FACTORS:<br />Ease of Reading: 3 Stars<br />Writing Style: 3 Stars<br />Characters and Character Development: 2 Stars<br />Plot Structure and Development: 3 Stars<br />Level of Captivation: 3 Stars<br />Originality: 2 Stars</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="6014394632299883080" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_202585087" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202585087" />
    <a title="Dfordoom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1048045-dfordoom"><img alt="Dfordoom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207231748p2/1048045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202585087?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dfordoom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dfordoom" href="/user/show/1048045-dfordoom">Dfordoom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1048045-dfordoom?shelf=crime-mystery">crime-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202585087" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5006327902922790971">Gaston Leroux is of course best known as the author of <em>Phantom of the Opera</em> but he was actually quite prolific. He write quite a few mysteries, the most famous being <em>The Mystery of the Yellow Room</em>.<br/><br/>This is the book that introduces his detective Rouletabille, and an interesting sleuth he is too. He is in fact a newspaper reporter rather than a detective as such but as a crime-solver he is second to none. The most interesting thing about him though is that he is just 18 years old. He’s a boy genius</span>
  <span id="freeText5006327902922790971" style="display:none">Gaston Leroux is of course best known as the author of <em>Phantom of the Opera</em> but he was actually quite prolific. He write quite a few mysteries, the most famous being <em>The Mystery of the Yellow Room</em>.<br /><br />This is the book that introduces his detective Rouletabille, and an interesting sleuth he is too. He is in fact a newspaper reporter rather than a detective as such but as a crime-solver he is second to none. The most interesting thing about him though is that he is just 18 years old. He’s a boy genius detective. Being so young his biggest problem is natural persuading people to take him seriously. He’s also very much a teenager, with the arrogance and impetuosity of youth, and inclined on occasion to the kinds of errors of judgment you’d expect from someone with limited experience of life. <br /><br />In spite of these minor weaknesses he is the foremost detective of his era, and already has some extraordinary successes behind him.<br /><br />The plot involves the attempted murder of a scientist’s daughter. Mademoiselle Stangerson has spent her entire adult life acting as her father’s assistant and at 35 remains unmarried. For years a fellow scientist has been paying court to her, but although she is fond of M Darzac she steadfastly refuses to marry him.<br /><br />Professor Stangerson and his daughter have been working in a fairly esoteric field of science, researching the dissolution of matter. There seems no obvious motive for the vicious assault upon her, an assault that leaves her close to death. Even more puzzling is the fact that her room, next door to their laboratory, is locked from the inside when her father and the servants finally break down the door after hearing sounds of a struggle and gunshots.<br /><br />This is an early example of the locked-room sub-genre, in which a crime is committed that appears to be impossible but nevertheless it has occurred. Edgar Allan Poe a the inventor of this sub-genre with his story <em>The Murders in the Rue Morgue</em> and Conan Doyle had also tried his hand at it with <em>The Speckled Band</em>. The potential problem with this type of mystery is that the solution to the crime often tends to be even more contrived than the usual run of detective stories.<br /><br /><em>The Mystery of the Yellow Room</em> goes perilously close to being just a little bit too clever for its own good. On the other hand it’s certainly ingenious and it’s lively and entertaining and on the whole it succeeds pretty well. Making allowances for the fact that fictional crimes are always much more convoluted than real-life crimes it has to be admitted that Leroux has come up with a plot of genuine originality and interest.<br /><br />The book was very highly regarded in its day and it’s easy to see why Leroux was so popular. It’s definitely worth picking up, and the Wordsworth paperback edition is appealingly inexpensive as well. It’s also a reminder of the very significant contribution that French authors made to the development of the crime novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5006327902922790971" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4157710796792992751">A young woman is found attacked and bleeding, while locked in an impenetrable room: door locked, windows barred. Who attacked her and how? Only the brilliant Rouletabille can solve the crime!<br/><br/>I hadn't realized this, but it turns out the author of The Phantom of the Opera, Gaston Leroux, was the Arthur Conan Doyle of France, writing a bajillion mysteries and other pulp novels. This is very readable. I had the mystery from early on--and then got completely talked out of my theory, spending the rest</span>
  <span id="freeText4157710796792992751" style="display:none">A young woman is found attacked and bleeding, while locked in an impenetrable room: door locked, windows barred. Who attacked her and how? Only the brilliant Rouletabille can solve the crime!<br /><br />I hadn't realized this, but it turns out the author of The Phantom of the Opera, Gaston Leroux, was the Arthur Conan Doyle of France, writing a bajillion mysteries and other pulp novels. This is very readable. I had the mystery from early on--and then got completely talked out of my theory, spending the rest of the book so far off track that I was in another genre. A fun read.</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2441165223" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer674120496524262401"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/188214.The_Mystery_of_the_Yellow_Room__Joseph_Rouletabille__1_' title='The Mystery of the Yellow Room (Joseph Rouletabille #1) by Gaston Leroux' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Mystery of the Yellow Room</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9057.Gaston_Leroux' title='Gaston Leroux' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gaston Leroux</a> was really kicking my butt and it took forever to finish this book as it just did not hold my interest. The crime is interesting (an attempted murder in a locked room) but the characters are incredibly annoying and arrogant (not the charmingly, over-the-top arrogance of Poirot!) and the writing style kept taking me out of the story. I've found that books written around this period (late 1800s/early 1900s) have a way of doing dialogue that I really </span>
  <span id="freeText674120496524262401" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/188214.The_Mystery_of_the_Yellow_Room__Joseph_Rouletabille__1_" title="The Mystery of the Yellow Room (Joseph Rouletabille #1) by Gaston Leroux" rel="nofollow noopener">The Mystery of the Yellow Room</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9057.Gaston_Leroux" title="Gaston Leroux" rel="nofollow noopener">Gaston Leroux</a> was really kicking my butt and it took forever to finish this book as it just did not hold my interest. The crime is interesting (an attempted murder in a locked room) but the characters are incredibly annoying and arrogant (not the charmingly, over-the-top arrogance of Poirot!) and the writing style kept taking me out of the story. I've found that books written around this period (late 1800s/early 1900s) have a way of doing dialogue that I really don't like. Everything seems to be written as an exclamation! Everything! Whether it needs it or not! And, it annoys me! :)<br /><br />Rating: 1.5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="674120496524262401" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17411780191263952352">Yet another locked room mystery. I'm just not very good at figuring these out, so I'm easily entertained. I did have some suspicions about parts of it, but even those were not quite on target.<br/><br/>In this first of the series, Rouletabille is an 18-year old journalist assigned to the case. His real name is not Rouletabille, but men have a penchant for giving people nicknames by which they are forever known. I am unable to find a translation for this word, but he was given it because his head is round </span>
  <span id="freeText17411780191263952352" style="display:none">Yet another locked room mystery. I'm just not very good at figuring these out, so I'm easily entertained. I did have some suspicions about parts of it, but even those were not quite on target.<br /><br />In this first of the series, Rouletabille is an 18-year old journalist assigned to the case. His real name is not Rouletabille, but men have a penchant for giving people nicknames by which they are forever known. I am unable to find a translation for this word, but he was given it because his head is round like a billiard ball. This round head houses a very observant and logical character.<br /><br />It's a short series, and has apparently been made into some movies or a TV series (not sure which). I'd happily read another installment.</span>

<div id="review_3459122271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3459122271" />
    <a title="Tanaya Deshmukh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42675803-tanaya-deshmukh"><img alt="Tanaya Deshmukh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602426915p2/42675803.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3459122271?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanaya Deshmukh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanaya Deshmukh" href="/user/show/42675803-tanaya-deshmukh">Tanaya Deshmukh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3459122271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4841998328658704036">I have been meaning to read this one for a long time after I heard it’s one of the best mysteries out there. I wish I would’ve had the same opinion but sadly I don’t. This book was really interesting at start but after few pages in it quickly became frustrating. The detective in this story god knows how knows who the culprit is but does not divulge any information no matter how many times the protagonist asks which was really irritating. I don’t expect the author to tell everything at the start,</span>
  <span id="freeText4841998328658704036" style="display:none">I have been meaning to read this one for a long time after I heard it’s one of the best mysteries out there. I wish I would’ve had the same opinion but sadly I don’t. This book was really interesting at start but after few pages in it quickly became frustrating. The detective in this story god knows how knows who the culprit is but does not divulge any information no matter how many times the protagonist asks which was really irritating. I don’t expect the author to tell everything at the start, obviously, but there is not much going on in the story which then started getting on my nerves. The story is so repetitive that I felt like I would never really reach the end.<br />The characters in this book for some really lame ass reason keep mum throughout the book even though their lives are at stake! I was dying to know the reason behind this and when I read it I couldn’t believe it. How can someone be okay with getting arrested instead of speaking about THAT. I would not want to give spoilers but I think this story would’ve been more of a sensation when it was written way back. I did not really understand the hype reading it now.<br />I also did not like the end of the story and I think this is more due to the fact that I waited a longggg time for it to come and hence really lost my excitement somewhere along the way. And also, due to this that I had already guessed it as one of the probable endings but was hoping for it to be anything but that!<br />Anyway, I would not recommend this one but people can still give it a try looking at how many people have loved it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4841998328658704036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Rob Baker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18084923-rob-baker"><img alt="Rob Baker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1382749423p2/18084923.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3388471330?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob Baker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob Baker" href="/user/show/18084923-rob-baker">Rob Baker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18084923-rob-baker?shelf=genre-fiction-independent-reading">genre-fiction-independent-reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3388471330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10703861984502900848">A thoroughly enjoyable classic locked-room mystery. <br/><br/>Leroux's delightful and brilliant journalist-cum-detective, Rouletabille--a latter-day Holmes/proto-Poirot--, exudes youthful confidence, flair, and bravado, making one fantastic and amusing pronouncement after another that keep his Watson (Sainclair) and the reader baffled and astounded. Leroux himself displays authorial panache by opening his story with a perplexing and intriguing mystery and then constantly raising the stakes with new intrig</span>
  <span id="freeText10703861984502900848" style="display:none">A thoroughly enjoyable classic locked-room mystery. <br /><br />Leroux's delightful and brilliant journalist-cum-detective, Rouletabille--a latter-day Holmes/proto-Poirot--, exudes youthful confidence, flair, and bravado, making one fantastic and amusing pronouncement after another that keep his Watson (Sainclair) and the reader baffled and astounded. Leroux himself displays authorial panache by opening his story with a perplexing and intriguing mystery and then constantly raising the stakes with new intrigue and surprises. <br /><br />The mystery and its solution include myriad clever--albeit often far-fetched--elements, but it's all done with such style, wit, and creativity that its unlikeliness and complexity are easily overlooked.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10703861984502900848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3046092003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6064097125508183527">It is the first time I read this thriller and I really enjoyed its rhythm, the strength of the main characters and the old minded, but still enchanting, mentality of the characters. About Joseph Rouletabile, one can say he is at the same time endearing, irritating and yet quite interesting detective! With his "good circle of reason" he surpasses many other detectives in thrllers...<br/>Definitely a good read!<br/><br/>Maria Carmo,<br/><br/>Lisbon, 2 December 2019. </span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CFhXLxvfv3hYSa7ZGdDGz7tDxQuHOQQq1ul4G41kQ45tiJIxlHr1VcpeaOMdn6iK5t3/RN40RTQW6pfogDtveQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bv4/uWPfZGkniLZnH9XGPVSQFHO4of37eMRb+Skm37ALLvqn7HouRLWfcF0bmqh4CQ4uPOGsvOW4x7QKJHnzRw==')})
  }

Needless<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6210094379420642326\" style=\"display:none\">Regarded as the first full-length locked room mystery, the novel focuses on a murder that has occurred inside a locked room, with no clear indication as to the weapon used, the perpetrator of the horrendous crime, or a possible escape route. Needless to say, The Big Bow Mystery has all the elements necessary to engage its audience and encourage them to look between the lines in an attempt to unravel the complex murder.Set in Bow, east London, the novel opens when Mrs. Drabdump, a widow who rents out rooms, panics when one of her lodgers does not respond to her fervent attempts to wake him. In a moment of distress, she seeks the help of her neighbor George Grodman, incidentally a former detective, who eventually forces his way inside the room. Consequently, they witness a gruesome sight, as they find Arthur Constant dead, his throat slashed. Astounded by the strange circumstances under which the murder has occurred, bearing in mind that the door and windows were locked and secured from the inside with no apparent way of leaving the crime scene, the case is truly baffling. Moreover, the motive for the murder is also a mystery, as Constant was a likeable man, known for his efforts to help the working class. The perplexing case is taken up by Grodman and Inspector Edward Wimp, who share a mutual detest, yet amusingly play along with an act of cordiality. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12679802615972399526"><b>Gaston Louis Alfred Leroux</b> was a French journalist and author of detective fiction.<br/><br/>In the English-speaking world, he is best known for writing the novel <i>The Phantom of the Opera</i> (<i>Le Fantôme de l'Opéra,</i> 1910), which has been made into several film and stage productions of the same name, such as the 1925 film starring Lon Chaney, and Andrew Lloyd Webber's 1986 musical. It was also the basis of the 1</span>
  <span id="freeText12679802615972399526" style="display:none"><b>Gaston Louis Alfred Leroux</b> was a French journalist and author of detective fiction.<br /><br />In the English-speaking world, he is best known for writing the novel <i>The Phantom of the Opera</i> (<i>Le Fantôme de l'Opéra,</i> 1910), which has been made into several film and stage productions of the same name, such as the 1925 film starring Lon Chaney, and Andrew Lloyd Webber's 1986 musical. It was also the basis of the 1990 novel <i>Phantom</i> by Susan Kay.<br /><br />Leroux went to school in Normandy and studied law in Paris, graduating in 1889. He inherited millions of francs and lived wildly until he nearly reached bankruptcy. Then in 1890, he began working as a court reporter and theater critic for <i>L'Écho de Paris.</i> His most important journalism came when he began working as an international correspondent for the Paris newspaper <i>Le Matin.</i> In 1905 he was present at and covered the Russian Revolution. Another case he was present at involved the investigation and deep coverage of an opera house in Paris, later to become a ballet house. The basement consisted of a cell that held prisoners in the Paris Commune, which were the rulers of Paris through much of the Franco-Prussian war.<br /><br />He suddenly left journalism in 1907, and began writing fiction. In 1909, he and Arthur Bernède formed their own film company, Société des Cinéromans to simultaneously publish novels and turn them into films. He first wrote a mystery novel entitled Le mystère de la chambre jaune (1908; The Mystery of the Yellow Room), starring the amateur detective Joseph Rouletabille. Leroux's contribution to French detective fiction is considered a parallel to Sir Arthur Conan Doyle's in the United Kingdom and Edgar Allan Poe's in America. Leroux died in Nice on April 15, 1927, of a urinary tract infection.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12679802615972399526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
